        <title>The Tetracentron genome provides insight into the early evolution of eudicots and the formation of vessel elements | Genome Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Tetracentron sinense is an endemic and endangered deciduous tree. It belongs to the Trochodendrales, one of four early diverging lineages of eudicots known for having vesselless secondary wood. Sequencing and resequencing of the T. sinense genome will help us understand eudicot evolution, the genetic basis of tracheary element development, and the genetic diversity of this relict species. Here, we report a chromosome-scale assembly of the T. sinense genome. We assemble the 1.07&#8201;Gb genome sequence into 24 chromosomes and annotate 32,690 protein-coding genes. Phylogenomic analyses verify that the Trochodendrales and core eudicots are sister lineages and showed that two whole-genome duplications occurred in the Trochodendrales approximately 82 and 59 million years ago. Synteny analyses suggest that the &#947; event, resulting in paleohexaploidy, may have only happened in core eudicots. Interestingly, we find that vessel elements are present in T. sinense, which has two orthologs of AtVND7, the master regulator of vessel formation. T. sinense also has several key genes regulated by or regulating TsVND7.2 and their regulatory relationship resembles that in Arabidopsis thaliana. Resequencing and population genomics reveals high levels of genetic diversity of T. sinense and identifies four refugia in China. The T. sinense genome provides a unique reference for inferring the early evolution of eudicots and the mechanisms underlying vessel element formation. Population genomics analysis of T. sinense reveals its genetic diversity and geographic structure with implications for conservation."/>

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <script>
        function OptanonWrapper() {
            var elementInside = function(candidate, element) {
                if (candidate === element) {
                    return true;
                } else if (candidate.nodeName.toLowerCase() === 'body') {
                    return false;
                } else {
                    return elementInside(candidate.parentNode, element);
                }
            };

            var disclaimer = document.querySelector('.c-disclaimer[aria-hidden="false"]');
            window.dataLayer.push({event:'OneTrustGroupsUpdated'});
            if (disclaimer) {
                if (!elementInside(document.activeElement, disclaimer)) {

                document.activeElement.blur();

        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Genome Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="The Tetracentron genome provides insight into the early evolution of eudicots and the formation of vessel elements"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Tetracentron sinense is an endemic and endangered deciduous tree. It belongs to the Trochodendrales, one of four early diverging lineages of eudicots known for having vesselless secondary wood. Sequencing and resequencing of the T. sinense genome will help us understand eudicot evolution, the genetic basis of tracheary element development, and the genetic diversity of this relict species. Here, we report a chromosome-scale assembly of the T. sinense genome. We assemble the 1.07Gb genome sequence into 24 chromosomes and annotate 32,690 protein-coding genes. Phylogenomic analyses verify that the Trochodendrales and core eudicots are sister lineages and showed that two whole-genome duplications occurred in the Trochodendrales approximately 82 and 59 million years ago. Synteny analyses suggest that the  event, resulting in paleohexaploidy, may have only happened in core eudicots. Interestingly, we find that vessel elements are present in T. sinense, which has two orthologs of AtVND7, the master regulator of vessel formation. T. sinense also has several key genes regulated by or regulating TsVND7.2 and their regulatory relationship resembles that in Arabidopsis thaliana. Resequencing and population genomics reveals high levels of genetic diversity of T. sinense and identifies four refugia in China. The T. sinense genome provides a unique reference for inferring the early evolution of eudicots and the mechanisms underlying vessel element formation. Population genomics analysis of T. sinense reveals its genetic diversity and geographic structure with implications for conservation."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/13059.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p><i>Tetracentron sinense</i> is an endemic and endangered deciduous tree. It belongs to the Trochodendrales, one of four early diverging lineages of eudicots known for having vesselless secondary wood. Sequencing and resequencing of the <i>T. sinense</i> genome will help us understand eudicot evolution, the genetic basis of tracheary element development, and the genetic diversity of this relict species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>Here, we report a chromosome-scale assembly of the <i>T. sinense</i> genome. We assemble the 1.07Gb genome sequence into 24 chromosomes and annotate 32,690 protein-coding genes. Phylogenomic analyses verify that the Trochodendrales and core eudicots are sister lineages and showed that two whole-genome duplications occurred in the Trochodendrales approximately 82 and 59 million years ago. Synteny analyses suggest that the  event, resulting in paleohexaploidy, may have only happened in core eudicots. Interestingly, we find that vessel elements are present in <i>T. sinense</i>, which has two orthologs of <i>AtVND7</i>, the master regulator of vessel formation. <i>T. sinense</i> also has several key genes regulated by or regulating <i>TsVND7.2</i> and their regulatory relationship resembles that in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana.</i> Resequencing and population genomics reveals high levels of genetic diversity of <i>T. sinense</i> and identifies four refugia in China.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>The <i>T. sinense</i> genome provides a unique reference for inferring the early evolution of eudicots and the mechanisms underlying vessel element formation. Population genomics analysis of <i>T. sinense</i> reveals its genetic diversity and geographic structure with implications for conservation.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Eudicots are the largest and most diverse group of angiosperms, containing 75% of all angiosperm species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Zeng L, Zhang Q, Sun R, Kong H, Zhang N, Ma H. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat Commun. 2014;5:4956." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1183">1</a>]. Recent phylogenetic studies identified several early diverging eudicot branches and a strongly supported clade called the core eudicots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bremer B, Bremer K, Chase MW, Fay MF, Reveal JL, Soltis DE, et al. An update of the angiosperm phylogeny group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG III. Bot J Linn Soc. 2009;161:10521." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1186">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Byng JW, Chase MW, Christenhusz MJM, Fay MF, Judd WS, Mabberley DJ, et al. An update of the angiosperm phylogeny group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot J Linn Soc. 2016;181:120." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1189">3</a>] comprising the majority of eudicot species. The rise and eventual dominance of the core eudicots may have benefited from the well-known  hexaploidization event in their most recent common ancestor [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Jiao Y, Leebens-Mack J, Ayyampalayam S, Bowers JE, Mckain MR, McNeal J, et al. A genome triplication associated with early diversification of the core eudicots. Genome Biol. 2012;13:R3." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1192">4</a>]. However, the timing and nature of the  event are still controversial, and a recent study proposed a two-step model for the  event, with the first whole-genome duplication occurring in the common ancestor of all eudicots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Akoez G, Nordborg M. The Aquilegia genome reveals a hybrid origin of core eudicots. Genome Biol. 2019;20:256." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1195">5</a>]. Sequencing more early diverging eudicots can help us understand the nature of the  event, as well as other genomic changes that occurred during the early diversification of eudicots.</p><p>Within eudicots, the four extant eudicot lineages that diverged the earliest are the Ranunculales, Proteales, Trochodendrales, and Buxales [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Soltis DE, Smith SA, Cellinese N, Wurdack KJ, Tank DC, Brockington SF, et al. Angiosperm phylogeny: 17 genes, 640 taxa. Am J Bot. 2011;98:70430." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1201">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ruhfel BR, Gitzendanner MA, Soltis PS, Soltis DE, Burleigh JG. From algae to angiosperms-inferring the phylogeny of green plants (Viridiplantae) from 360 plastid genomes. BMC Evol Biol. 2014;14:23." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1204">7</a>]. The order Trochodendrales consists of one family (Trochodendraceae) and two species (<i>Tetracentron sinense</i> and <i>Trochodendron aralioides</i>). Although there has been controversy about the phylogenetic position of the Trochodendrales [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Endress PK. Floral structure, systematics, and phylogeny in Trochodendrales. Ann Mo Bot Gard. 1986;73:297324." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1213">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Doweld AB. Carpology, seed anatomy and taxonomic relationships of Tetracentron (Tetracentraceae) and Trochodendron (Trochodendraceae). Ann Bot-London. 1998;82:41343." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1217">9</a>], most phylogenetic analyses using organellar and nuclear genes strongly support the placement of Trochodendrales as early diverging eudicots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Soltis DE, Smith SA, Cellinese N, Wurdack KJ, Tank DC, Brockington SF, et al. Angiosperm phylogeny: 17 genes, 640 taxa. Am J Bot. 2011;98:70430." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1220">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ruhfel BR, Gitzendanner MA, Soltis PS, Soltis DE, Burleigh JG. From algae to angiosperms-inferring the phylogeny of green plants (Viridiplantae) from 360 plastid genomes. BMC Evol Biol. 2014;14:23." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1223">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chase MW, Soltis DE, Olmstead RG, Morgan D. Phylogenetics of seed plants: an analysis of nucleotide sequences from the plastid gene rbcL. Ann Mo Bot Gard. 1993;80:52880." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1226">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Soltis DE, Soltis PS, Nickrent DL, Johnson LA, Hahn WJ, Hoot SB, et al. Angiosperm phylogeny inferred from 18s ribosomal DNA sequences. Ann Mo Bot Gard. 1997;84:149." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1229">11</a>]. Whole-genome sequences from several Ranunculales and Proteales are available [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Akoez G, Nordborg M. The Aquilegia genome reveals a hybrid origin of core eudicots. Genome Biol. 2019;20:256." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1232">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Guo LR, Winzer T, Yang X, Li Y, Ning Z, He Z, et al. The opium poppy genome and morphinan production. Science. 2018;362:3437." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1236">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ming R, Leebens-Mack J, Ayyampalayam S, Bowers JE, McKain MR, McNeal J, et al. Genome of the long-living sacred lotus (Nelumbo nucifera Gaertn.). Genome Biol. 2013;14:R41." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1239">13</a>], genomes from Trochodendrales such as <i>T. sinense</i> will facilitate the comparative genomic analyses and shed deeper sight into the early evolution of eudicots.</p><p>Xylem, the water-conducting tissue in vascular plants, is composed of different cell types. The tracheids and vessel elements are conducting cells of the xylem [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ohashi-Ito K, Iwamoto K, Fukuda H. LOB domaincontaining protein 15 positively regulates expression of VND7, a master regulator of tracheary elements. Plant Cell Physiol. 2018;59:98996." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1248">14</a>]. Vessel elements are one of the most important features of angiosperms and differentiate them from other vascular plants such as gymnosperms (except gnetophytes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wan T, Liu Z-M, Li L-F, Leitch AR, Leitch IJ, Lohaus R, et al. A genome for gnetophytes and early evolution of seed plants. Nat Plants. 2018;4(2):829." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1251">15</a>]), which lack vessel elements. As they differentiate, vessel elements produce secondary cell walls and eventually undergo programmed cell death (PCD), forming a hollow tube that conducts water from the roots to the apical parts of the plant [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ohashi-Ito K, Iwamoto K, Fukuda H. LOB domaincontaining protein 15 positively regulates expression of VND7, a master regulator of tracheary elements. Plant Cell Physiol. 2018;59:98996." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1254">14</a>]. For almost 150years, <i>T. sinense</i> was considered to produce secondary xylem that has tracheids but lacks vessel elements [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bailey IW, Thompson WP. Additional notes upon the angiosperms Tetracentron, Trochodendron, and Drimys, in which vessels are absent from the wood. Ann Bot. 1918;32:50312." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1260">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Suzuki M, Joshi L, Fujii T, Noshiro S. The anatomy of unusual tracheids in Tetracentron wood. IAWA Bulletin. 1991;12:2333." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1260_1">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Carlquist S. Pit membrane remnants in perforation plates of primitive dicotyledons and their significance. Am J Bot. 1992;79:66070." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1264">18</a>]. However, some studies suggest that vessel elements are present in the secondary xylem of <i>T. sinense</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ren Y, Chen L, Tian XH, Zhang XH, Lu AM. Discovery of vessels in Tetracentron (Trochodendraceae) and its systematic significance. Plant Syst Evol. 2007;267:15561." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1270">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li H-F, Chaw S-M, Du C-M, Ren Y. Vessel elements present in the secondary xylem of Trochodendron and Tetracentron (Trochodendraceae). Flora. 2011;206(6):595600." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1273">20</a>]. Therefore, whether the secondary xylem of <i>T. sinense</i> contains vessel elements remains an outstanding question that can be addressed by morphological studies, and examination of the genetic network that specifies vessel elements.</p><p>VASCULAR RELATED NAC-DOMAIN PROTEIN 7 (VND7) functions as a master regulator of vessel element formation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Yamaguchi M, Mitsuda N, Ohtani M, Ohme-Takagi M, Kato K, Demura T. VASCULAR-RELATED NAC-DOMAIN 7 directly regulates the expression of a broad range of genes for xylem vessel formation. Plant J. 2011;66:57990." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1282">21</a>]. VND7 directly regulates many genes such as the transcription factor genes <i>MYB46</i> and <i>MYB83</i>, the cellulose synthase (CES) genes <i>CESA4</i> and <i>CESA8</i>, and the xylem-specific papain-like cysteine peptidase (XCP) genes <i>XCP1</i> and <i>XCP2</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhong R, Richardson EA, Ye ZH. The MYB46 transcription factor is a direct target of SND1 and regulates secondary wall biosynthesis in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell. 2007;19:277692." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1304">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="McCarthy RL, Zhong R, Ye ZH. MYB83 is a direct target of SND1 and acts redundantly with MYB46 in the regulation of secondary cell wall biosynthesis in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell Physiol. 2009;50:195064." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1304_1">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim WC, Ko JH, Kim JY, Kim J, Bae HJ, Han KH. MYB46 directly regulates the gene expression of secondary wall-associated cellulose synthases in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 2013;73:2636." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1304_2">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Somerville C. Cellulose synthesis in higher plants. Annu Rev Cell Dev Biol. 2006;22:5378." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1304_3">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Funk V, Kositsup B, Zhao C, Beers EP. The Arabidopsis xylem peptidase XCP1 is a tracheary element vacuolar protein that may be a papain ortholog. Plant Physiol. 2002;128:8494." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1307">26</a>]. These genes mediate secondary cell wall formation and PCD, the two essential processes of vessel element differentiation. <i>VND7</i> is positively regulated by several factors, including the LOB DOMAIN-CONTAINING PROTEINs (LBDs) LBD18 and LBD30, and VND1VND5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ohashi-Ito K, Iwamoto K, Fukuda H. LOB domaincontaining protein 15 positively regulates expression of VND7, a master regulator of tracheary elements. Plant Cell Physiol. 2018;59:98996." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1313">14</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Soyano T, Thitamadee S, Machida Y, Chua NH. ASYMMETRIC LEAVES2-LIKE19/LATERAL ORGAN BOUNDARIES DOMAIN30 and ASL20/LBD18 regulate tracheary element differentiation in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell. 2008;20:335973." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1317">27</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Endo H, Yamaguchi M, Tamura T, Nakano Y, Nishikubo N, Yoneda A, et al. Multiple classes of transcription factors regulate the expression of VASCULAR-RELATED NAC-DOMAIN7, a master switch of xylem vessel differentiation. Plant Cell Physiol. 2015;56:24254." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1320">28</a>]. Whether an ortholog of <i>VND7</i> is present in <i>T. sinense</i> and whether the regulatory network of <i>T. sinense VND7</i> parallels the <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> regulatory network remain unknown.</p><p><i>T. sinense</i> (2n=48 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ratter JA, Milne C. Chromosome numbers of some primitive angiosperms. Notes from the Royal Botanic Garden, Edinburgh. 1973;32:4238." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1341">29</a>]) is a tertiary relict species that is mainly found in eastern Asia, including eastern Nepal and southwestern and central China [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pigg KB, Wehr WC, Ickert-Bond SM. Trochodendron and nordenskioldia (Trochodendraceae) from the middle eocene of Washington State, USA. Int J Plant Sci. 2011;162:118798." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1344">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sun Y, Moore MJ, Yue L, Feng T, Chu H, Chen S, et al. Chloroplast phylogeography of the East Asian Arcto-Tertiary relict Tetracentron sinense (Trochodendraceae). J Biogeogr. 2014;41:172132." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1344_1">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Li S, Gan X, Han H, Zhang X, Tian Z. Low within-population genetic diversity and high genetic differentiation among populations of the endangered plant Tetracentron sinense Oliver revealed by inter-simple sequence repeat analysis. Ann For Sci. 2018;75:74." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1347">32</a>]. The climate change during the Pleistocene ice age and human disturbances in recent years resulted in a sharp decline in <i>T. sinense</i> population size [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Li S, Gan X, Han H, Zhang X, Tian Z. Low within-population genetic diversity and high genetic differentiation among populations of the endangered plant Tetracentron sinense Oliver revealed by inter-simple sequence repeat analysis. Ann For Sci. 2018;75:74." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1353">32</a>]. <i>T. sinense</i> has been documented as an endangered species in China and is currently listed in Appendix III of the Convention of International Trade of Endangered Species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sun Y, Moore MJ, Yue L, Feng T, Chu H, Chen S, et al. Chloroplast phylogeography of the East Asian Arcto-Tertiary relict Tetracentron sinense (Trochodendraceae). J Biogeogr. 2014;41:172132." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1360">31</a>]. Although the evolutionary history of <i>T. sinense</i> has been investigated by examination of a few plastid regions and inter-simple sequence repeat analysis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sun Y, Moore MJ, Yue L, Feng T, Chu H, Chen S, et al. Chloroplast phylogeography of the East Asian Arcto-Tertiary relict Tetracentron sinense (Trochodendraceae). J Biogeogr. 2014;41:172132." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1366">31</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Li S, Gan X, Han H, Zhang X, Tian Z. Low within-population genetic diversity and high genetic differentiation among populations of the endangered plant Tetracentron sinense Oliver revealed by inter-simple sequence repeat analysis. Ann For Sci. 2018;75:74." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1369">32</a>], controversies remain, including the identification of potential refugia. In addition, little is known about the genome-scale genetic diversity and the population dynamics of this species in response to climate change, from the Pleistocene to the present. Genome-wide resequencing of <i>T. sinense</i> accessions from different populations would help us assess the levels and patterns of genetic variation and the evolutionary history of this endangered species, providing a practical strategy for its conservation.</p><p>Here we report the sequencing, assembly, and annotation of the <i>T. sinense</i> genome and its resequencing in key accessions. Our major objectives were (1) to explore the genomic changes that occurred during the early evolution of eudicots and core eudicots, (2) to investigate whether <i>T. sinense</i> has vessel elements in its secondary xylem and whether it has VND7 (and if so, whether the VND7 regulatory network is as in <i>A. thaliana</i>), and (3) to investigate the genetic diversity and evolutionary history of <i>T. sinense</i>. Our results reveal the role of polyploidization in the early evolution of eudicots and the nature of the known  event, as well as exploring other genomic changes that occurred during eudicot diversification. Our genome-wide resequencing provided further insight into the evolutionary history of <i>T. sinense</i>. Moreover, light microscopy and the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) observations of slices of <i>T. sinense</i> wood and analyses of X-ray computed microtomography (microCT) support the idea that vessel elements are present in the secondary xylem of <i>T. sinense</i>. We also investigated the genetic basis of the development of vessel elements by identifying <i>T. sinense</i> orthologs of <i>VND7</i> and exploring their regulation. Therefore, our results provide genomic insight on the evolution of this intriguing species and on the evolution of xylem vessels.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">SMRT sequence and assembly of the <i>T. sinense</i> genome</h3><p>To analyze the genome of <i>T. sinense</i>, we first generated 82G Illumina paired-end reads (150bp). The genome size of the sequenced individual was estimated to be ~1.12Gb, and its heterozygosity rates were estimated to be 0.22%, based on <i>k</i>-mer analysis (<i>k</i>-mer frequency distributions) (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S1). We then used a combination of single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing technology from Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) and high-throughput chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) to produce the final sequenced and assembled genome of <i>T. sinense</i>. In total, we generated 14.8M PacBio single-molecule long reads (average read length 8kb, longest read 84kb), for 118Gb total sequence, giving ~100 coverage of the assembled genome. De novo assembly yielded 3389 contigs, with a contig N50 length of 1.99Mb, which was further improved to 2.8Mb with Gapcloser software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Xu GC, Xu TJ, Zhu R, Zhang Y, Li SQ, Wang HW, et al. LR_Gapcloser: A tiling pathbased gap closer that uses long reads to complete genome assembly. Gigascience. 2019;8: &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/gigascience/giy157&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1438">33</a>]. The total assembly size was 1.17Gb, which is consistent with the genome size estimated by <i>k</i>-mer analysis (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S1).</p><p>To further refine the <i>T. sinense</i> assembly, Hi-C libraries were constructed and sequenced. The Hi-C read pairs were mapped onto the draft assembly (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S2), yielding an assembly with a scaffold N50 of 45Mb. The final reference assembly comprised 24 chromosome-scale pseudomolecules hereafter referred to as chromosomes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S2b), with maximum and minimum lengths of 65Mb and 33Mb, respectively. The total length of the chromosomes accounted for 92.24% (1.07Gb) of the assembled genome size of 1.17Gb. The assembled chromosome number is equal to previous estimates of the haploid chromosome number of <i>T. sinense</i> (2n=48). BUSCO [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Simao FR, Waterhouse M, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:32102." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1462">34</a>] assessment showed that 93.4% complete genes were captured.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat and gene annotation</h3><p>We identified 787Mb of repetitive sequences in the genome of <i>T. sinense</i>, accounting for 67.8% of the genomic assembly. Transposable elements were the predominant component (65.9%), with the long terminal repeat (LTR) family being the largest part (32.2%) of these transposons. Within the LTR family, the <i>Gypsy</i> subfamily was the most abundant, making up 20.5% of the genome, followed by the <i>Copia</i> subfamily (11%) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1).</p><p>Based on the remaining repeat-masked <i>T. sinense</i> genome, we annotated 32,690 protein-coding gene models by combining ab initio gene predictions and evidence-based gene annotations using the MAKER pipeline. These gene models had an average transcript length of 1492bp and an average coding-sequence length of 1304bp. The gene models contained an average of 5.4 exons per gene with average exon and intron lengths of 153bp and 283bp, respectively. In addition, we identified 166 rRNAs, 811 tRNAs, and 1062 non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs). A BUSCO assessment based on conserved plant gene models identified 93.8% complete gene models.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Phylogenomic and gene family evolution analyses</h3><p>To ascertain the phylogenetic position of <i>T. sinense</i> and the Trochodendrales, we performed phylogenomic analysis using 214 single-copy gene families (orthogroups) from 19 sequenced angiosperm genomes identified by OrthoFinder v2.3.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Emms DM, Kelly S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 2015;16:157. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1186/s13059-015-0721-2&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1502">35</a>]. These orthologous groups with single-copy genes were concatenated to construct a maximum likelihood (ML) phylogenetic tree using IQ-TREE. As shown in the ML tree (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig1">1</a>), the major lineages of angiosperms (monocots, magnoliids, and eudicots) were all recovered as monophyletic groups with 100% support. In eudicots, the core eudicot species formed a strongly supported monophyletic clade (100% bootstrap support). The Ranunculales, Proteales, and Trochodendrales formed successive sister clades to the core eudicots with 100% support, consistent with previous analyses [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Soltis DE, Smith SA, Cellinese N, Wurdack KJ, Tank DC, Brockington SF, et al. Angiosperm phylogeny: 17 genes, 640 taxa. Am J Bot. 2011;98:70430." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1508">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ruhfel BR, Gitzendanner MA, Soltis PS, Soltis DE, Burleigh JG. From algae to angiosperms-inferring the phylogeny of green plants (Viridiplantae) from 360 plastid genomes. BMC Evol Biol. 2014;14:23." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1511">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Li HT, Yi TS, Gao ML, Ma PF, Zhang T, Yang JB, et al. Origin of angiosperms and the puzzle of the jurassic gap. Nat Plants. 2019;5:46170." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1515">36</a>].
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="560" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Tree and phylogenetic tree. <b>a</b>, <b>b</b> A typical mature tree of <i>Tetracentron sinense.</i> <b>c</b> Phylogenetic tree of nineteen species including divergence times based on 214 single-copy nuclear genes. Divergence times are indicated by the numbers around each node with 95% highest posterior density. The red dot represents a calibration point. All nodes have 100% bootstrap support in the Maximum Likelihood analysis. Light blue indicates core eudicots, dark blue indicates early diverging eudicots, orange indicates magnoliids, green indicates monocots, and light red indicates basal angiosperms. Green, red, and blue numbers above branches indicate the number of gene families that have expanded, contracted, and rapidly evolved (<i>p</i> value 0.01), respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We further estimated the divergence times of eudicot lineages using the MCMCTree [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Yang ZH, Rannala B. Bayesian estimation of species divergence times under a molecular clock using multiple fossil calibrations with soft bounds. Mol Biol Evol. 2006;23:21226." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1554">37</a>] and BEAST 2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Bouckaert R, Heled J, Khnert D, Vaughan T, Wu CH, et al. BEAST 2: a software platform for bayesian evolutionary analysis. PLoS Comput Biol. 2014;10:e1003537." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1557">38</a>] programs; these two methods yielded consistent divergence times with only small differences (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig1">1</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S3). According to the MCMCTree analysis (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig1">1</a>), crown-group eudicots arose 136 million years ago (MYA; 129140 MYA, 95% highest posterior density). Apart from the Ranunculales, the earliest divergences within the eudicots based on our analyses were the split of the Proteales and Trochodendrales from the rest of the eudicots at ~131 (123137) MYA and ~125 (117132) MYA, respectively. In our analysis, clades corresponding to the core eudicots originated at ~113 (105123) MYA. Within the Trochodendrales, the divergence of its two species was estimated to have occurred at 31 (1184) MYA.</p><p>We also investigated gene family evolution during the early diversification of eudicots. According to a CAFE analysis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="De Bie T, Cristianini N, Demuth JP, Hahn MW. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics. 2006;22:126971." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1572">39</a>], five times as many gene families expanded in the lineage leading to all eudicots (551), as compared to core eudicots (110). However, twice as many gene families contracted in the lineage leading to core eudicots compared with the lineage leading to all eudicots (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig1">1</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S4), consistent with a number of characteristics shared by eudicots. We found that in the lineages leading to eudicots, core eudicots, and the most recent ancestor of Trochodendrales, some defense gene families evolved rapidly. In the lineage leading to core eudicots, the TM3 MAD-box gene family that includes the important flowering time gene <i>SUPPRESSOR OF OVEREXPRESSION OF CONSTANS1</i> has expanded (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S5a).</p><p>In the lineage leading to the common ancestor of the Trochodendrales, there was much more gene family expansion (3524) compared to gene family contraction (938). To the genome of <i>T. sinense</i>, 2229 and 1784 gene families had undergone expansion and contraction, respectively. Among these families, 168 evolved rapidly. In contrast, more gene families expanded (3058) and fewer gene families (1153) contracted in <i>T. trochodendron.</i> The rapidly evolving gene families of <i>T. sinense</i> include many genes enriched in functions related to defense, toxin catabolic process, response to salicylic acid, and oxidative stress (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">2</a>). These genes mainly belong to gene families, such as the defense-related <i>NBS-LRR</i> (nucleotide-binding site leucine-rich repeat), lectin <i>RLK</i> (receptor-like kinase), and <i>GST</i> (glutathione transferase) gene families (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S5b). The gene families involved in terpenoid biosynthesis also evolved rapidly (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2). Furthermore, the <i>ALG24</i> MAD-box genes expanded rapidly in <i>T. sinense</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S5c). In agreement with the enrichment of GO terms, the KEGG enrichment suggested that genes involved in plantpathogen interaction, glutathione metabolism, and terpenoid biosynthesis evolved rapidly in the <i>T. sinense</i> genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S6).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Whole-genome duplications</h3><p>Whole-genome duplications (WGDs, polyploidization) have played a major role in the evolutionary history of the angiosperms. Intragenomic syntenic analysis of <i>T. sinense</i> showed strong collinearity between large segments of chromosomes, such as 2, 5, 6, and 24; 4, 9, 15, and 19; 4, 10, 18, and 23; 7, 11, 12, and 13; and 8, 14, 16, and 20 (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig2">2</a>a). The widespread occurrence of one-versus-three syntenic blocks suggested that two WGD events (named  and ) have occurred in the genome of <i>T. sinense</i>.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="900" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Two WGDs occurred in the Trochodendrales. <b>a</b> Dot plot showing the DNA sequence alignment of 24 chromosomes in <i>T. sinense</i>. Dots representing the position of paralogous gene-pairs were colored according to the <i>Ks</i> color scale. <b>b</b> Syntenic pattern among genomic regions in <i>A. trichopoda</i>, <i>T. sinense</i>, and <i>N. colorata</i>. Each <i>Amborella</i> region aligns with up to with four regions in <i>Tetracentron</i> that resulted from two WGDs in the early Trochodendrales. Four homologous <i>Tetracentron</i> regions derived from two WGDs align with two distinct <i>N. colorata</i> regions. Examples are highlighted in color in plots <b>b</b>, <b>c</b>, and <b>d</b>. <b>c</b> Syntenic pattern among genomic regions in <i>T. sinense</i> and <i>C. kanehirae</i>. <b>d</b> Syntenic pattern among genomic regions in <i>A. coerulea</i>, <i>N. nucifera</i>, <i>T. sinense</i>, and <i>V. vinifera</i>. <b>e</b> Synonymous substitution rate (<i>Ks</i>) distributions of syntenic blocks for the <i>T. sinense</i> paralogs and orthologs with other species. <b>f</b> <i>Ks</i> distributions of syntenic blocks for paralogs attributed to the  and  WGDs in the Trochodendrales, and orthologs between the two species of Trochodendrales without out-paralogs. <b>g</b> Summary of polyploidy events in the history of eudicots</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>T. aralioides</i> is a sister of <i>T. sinense</i>. To investigate whether <i>T. aralioides</i> also experienced WGDs, we performed an intragenomic analysis of this species. Indeed, two WGDs also occurred in <i>T. aralioides</i> since intragenome comparisons showed a 1:3 syntenic depth ratio (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S7a)<i>.</i> To explore if the WGDs are common or lineage-specific, we performed intergenomic syntenic analyses between <i>T. sinense</i> and <i>T. aralioides.</i> The results showed a 1:1 syntenic relationship between <i>T. sinense</i> and <i>T. aralioides</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S7b), suggesting that the two WGDs are common to the Trochodendrales.</p><p>Furthermore, we assessed the synteny between <i>T. sinense</i> or <i>T. aralioides</i> and <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, which is the only living representative of the sister lineage to all other extant flowering plants and did not experience lineage-specific WGD events [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Albert VA, Barbazuk WB, de Pamphilis CW, Der JP L-MJ, Ma H, et al. The amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science. 2013;342:1241089." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1805">40</a>]. We identified a 1:4 syntenic depth ratio in the <i>A. trichopoda</i><i>T. sinense</i> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig2">2</a>b; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S8a) and <i>A. trichopoda</i><i>T. aralioides</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S8b) comparisons. We also performed intergenomic comparisons between <i>T. sinense</i>/<i>T. aralioides</i> and several species including the basal angiosperm <i>Nymphaea colorata</i>, the magnoliid <i>Cinnamomum kanehirae</i>, two early diverging eudicots (<i>Aquilegia coerulea</i> and <i>Nelumbo nucifera</i>), and the core eudicot <i>Vitis vinifera.</i> Genomic comparisons of <i>T. sinense</i>/<i>T. aralioides</i> and <i>N. colorata</i> showed a 4:2 syntenic pattern, and comparisons of <i>T. sinense</i>/<i>T. aralioides</i> and <i>C. kanehirae</i> showed a 4:4 syntenic pattern (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig2">2</a>b, c; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S8c-f). Given the known lineage-specific WGD in <i>N. colorata</i> and two lineage-specific WGDs in <i>C. kanehirae</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zhang L, Chen F, Zhang X, Li Z, Zhao Y, Lohaus R, et al. The water lily genome and the early evolution of flowering plants. Nature. 2020;577:7984." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1884">41</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Chaw S-M, Liu Y-C, Wu Y-W, Wang H-Y, Lin C-YI WC-S, et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat Plants. 2019;5:6373." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1888">42</a>], our results suggested that the two WGDs in Trochodendrales occurred after the divergence of eudicots and magnoliids.</p><p>In addition, the synteny relationship between <i>N. nucifera</i> and <i>N. colorata</i> and between <i>N. nucifera</i> and <i>C. kanehirae</i> is 2:2 and 2:4, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S8g, h), indicating one WGD in <i>N. nucifera</i> after the divergence from <i>N. colorata</i> and <i>C. kanehirae.</i> Several earlier studies showed that the WGD in <i>N. nucifera</i> is lineage-specific rather than eudicot-wide, rejecting the possibility that any WGD occurred in the common ancestor of all extant eudicots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ming R, Leebens-Mack J, Ayyampalayam S, Bowers JE, McKain MR, McNeal J, et al. Genome of the long-living sacred lotus (Nelumbo nucifera Gaertn.). Genome Biol. 2013;14:R41." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1922">13</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Gui S, Peng J, Wang X, Wu Z, Cao R, Salse J, et al. Improving Nelumbo nucifera genome assemblies using high-resolution genetic maps and BioNano genome mapping reveals ancient chromosome rearrangements. Plant J. 2018;94:72134." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1925">43</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Shi T, Rahmani RS, Gugger PF, Wang M, Li H, Zhang Y, et al. Distinct expression and methylation patterns for genes with different fates following a single whole-genome duplication in flowering plants. Mol Biol Evol. 2020;37:2394413." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d33374e1929">44</a>]. The collinear relationships between <i>T. sinense</i>/<i>T. aralioides</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i>, <i>N. nucifera</i>, and <i>V. vinifera</i> are 4: 2, 4:2, and 4:3 (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig2">2</a>d; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S9), respectively, consistent with independent WGDs in the respective lineages of the Trochodendrales, <i>A. coerulea</i>, <i>N. nucifera</i>, and <i>V. vinifera.</i></p><p>Distributions of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>Ks</i>) were further analyzed to confirm the WGDs in Trochodendrales and other early diverging lineages of eudicots. The <i>Ks</i> distributions of <i>T. sinense</i> paralogs and <i>T. aralioides</i> paralogs in syntenic regions showed two overlapping peaks supporting two WGDs in a common ancestor of <i>T. sinense</i> and <i>T. aralioid</i>es (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig2">2</a>e). As shown in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig2">2</a>e, the peaks of <i>Ks</i> distribution of <i>T. sinense/T. aralioides</i> paralogs (retained from two WGD events) are much smaller than those of orthologs comparing <i>T. sinense/T. aralioides</i> to <i>A. coerulea</i> and to <i>N. nucifera</i>, strongly suggesting the two WGDs in <i>T. sinense/T. aralioides</i> occurred after their divergence from <i>A. coerulea</i> and <i>N. nucifera</i>. In addition, the <i>N. nucifera</i> paralogs displayed lower peak <i>Ks</i> value than orthologs comparing <i>N. nucifera</i> to <i>T. sinense/T. aralioides</i> and to <i>A. coerulea</i>, further confirming that the WGD in <i>N. nucifera</i> is also lineage-specific.</p><p>According to the synteny analysis, we investigated the <i>Ks</i> distributions for paralogous gene pairs that originated from the two WGD events (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig2">2</a>f). Using divergence time and median <i>Ks</i> values of syntenic blocks between <i>T. sinense</i> and <i>T. aralioides</i>, we estimated the synonymous substitutions per site per year as 3.25e9 for the Trochodendrales (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>). Using this rate, we estimated that the  and  WGD events of Trochodendrales occurred at around 82 MYA and 59 MYA according to the median <i>Ks</i> of <i>T. sinense</i> paralogs, and 78 MYA and 54 MYA according to the median <i>Ks</i> of <i>T. aralioides</i> paralogs. This dating again suggests that the two WGDs occurred after the divergence of Trochodendrales from Proteales (<i>N. nucifera</i>).</p><p>Lastly, we performed integrated syntenic and phylogenomic analyses to confirm the timing of these WGDs in early diverging eudicots. We obtained anchor genes from the inter-genomic synteny blocks with ratio of 1:2:4 in <i>A. trichopoda</i>, <i>N. nucifera</i>, and <i>T. sinense</i>. All genes in the same sets of syntenic block were concatenated and used to construct phylogenetic trees. In total, we obtained 31 groups of concatenated genes (a total of 94 genes) and 87% of the trees based on the concatenated genes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S10) supported the hypothesis that the WGDs in <i>N. nucifera</i> and <i>T. sinense</i> are lineage-specific.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Occurrence of vessel elements in secondary xylem</h3><p>Under a light microscope, we found there was a distinct growth ring delineated by a band of latewood in the cross-section. Earlywood cells were larger and thin-walled, while those in latewood were smaller and thick-walled as reported by the previous study [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Suzuki M, Joshi L, Fujii T, Noshiro S. The anatomy of unusual tracheids in Tetracentron wood. IAWA Bulletin. 1991;12:2333." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2103">17</a>]. Occasionally, we noticed some peculiar cells occurred through the growth ring boundaries with much larger diameter (56.00.82m , <i>n</i>=35) than normal cells (44.70.98m, <i>n</i>=35) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig3">3</a>a). In the radial section, the peculiar cells had oblique end-walls (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig3">3</a>b). In the tangential section, we can clearly observe there are two types of tracheary elements. Most of the cells appeared as fibrous with 3.04.8mm in length while the peculiar cells were much shorter ranging from 308 to 597m and present as fusiform cells with many pits (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig3">3</a>c). Under scanning electron microscope (SEM), we observed the normal cells often have scalariform bordered pits on radial walls, sometimes with pit membrane remnants that are like a fibrillar mesh as reported previously (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig3">3</a>d, e). Using X-ray computed microtomography (microCT), we made a 3-dimensional reconstruction of different cells based on 1032 serial slice images and found the short and fusiform peculiar cells are sporadically present in <i>T. sinense</i> wood (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig3">3</a>fj). Therefore, we considered the larger and shorter peculiar cells as vessel elements, supporting the idea that vessel elements are present in <i>T. sinense</i>, although its vessel elements are primitive [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ren Y, Chen L, Tian XH, Zhang XH, Lu AM. Discovery of vessels in Tetracentron (Trochodendraceae) and its systematic significance. Plant Syst Evol. 2007;267:15561." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2134">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li H-F, Chaw S-M, Du C-M, Ren Y. Vessel elements present in the secondary xylem of Trochodendron and Tetracentron (Trochodendraceae). Flora. 2011;206(6):595600." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2138">20</a>].
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="637" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Occurrence of vessel elements in secondary xylem. <b>a</b><b>c</b> Light micrographs of wood sections from transverse, radial, and tangential sections of <i>T. sinense</i>, respectively. In the transverse sections, radial files of the peculiar cells are indicated by the arrows. Bar=200 and 50m in <b>a</b>, <b>c</b>, and <b>b</b>. <b>d</b> Scanning electron micrographs of the radial surface in <i>T. sinense</i>, showing scalariform pits. Scale bar=20m. <b>e</b> Scalariform pits with fibrillar mesh membrane remnants. Scale bar=10m. <b>f</b><b>i</b> Three-dimensional (3D) images of X-ray computed microtomography (microCT). <b>f</b> Original rendering of the 3D microCT image in <i>T. sinense</i>. <b>g</b><b>i</b> Images of radial (<i>y</i><i>z</i>) and tangential (<i>x</i><i>y</i>) sections of 3D microCT image at 3 different longitudinal positions of transverse sections (<i>x</i><i>z</i>). Arrows in <b>g</b> indicate the peculiar cells appearing in the tangential section. <b>j</b> Enlargement of <b>h</b> showing the short and fusiform peculiar cells. Light green dotted lines represent these peculiar cells. Scale bars are indicated by axis labels in <b>f</b><b>h</b> and 200m in <b>j</b></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">The evolution of <i>VNS</i>, <i>LBD</i>, <i>R2R3-MYB</i>, <i>CesA</i>, and <i>PLCP</i> gene families which are involved in the formation of vessel elements</h3><p><i>AtVND7</i>, <i>AtLBD18/AtLBD30</i>, <i>AtMYB46</i>/<i>AtMYB83</i>, <i>AtCESA4</i> and <i>AtCESA8</i>, and <i>AtXCP1</i>/<i>AtXCP2</i>, belonging to the <i>VNS</i>, <i>LBD</i>, <i>R2R3-MYB</i>, <i>CesA</i>, and <i>PLCP</i> gene families [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Somerville C. Cellulose synthesis in higher plants. Annu Rev Cell Dev Biol. 2006;22:5378." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2307">25</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ohtani M, Nishikubo N, Xu B, Yamaguchi M, Mitsuda N, Goue N, et al. A NAC domain protein family contributing to the regulation of wood formation in poplar. Plant J. 2011;67:499512." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2310">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Matsumura Y, Iwakawa H, Machida Y, Machida C. Characterization of genes in the ASYMMETRIC LEAVES2/LATERAL ORGAN BOUNDARIES (AS2/LOB) family in Arabidopsis thaliana, and functional and molecular comparisons between AS2 and other family members. Plant J. 2009;58:52537." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2310_1">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dubos C, Stracke R, Grotewold E, Weisshaar B, Martin C, Lepiniec L. MYB transcription factors in Arabidopsis. Trends Plant Sci. 2010;15:57381." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2310_2">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Richau KH, Kaschani F, Verdoes M, Pansuriya TC, Niessen S, Stueber K, et al. Subclassification and biochemical analysis of plant papain-like cysteine proteases displays subfamily-specific characteristics. Plant Physiol. 2012;158:158399." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2313">48</a>], respectively, are key genes involved in the development of vessel elements in <i>A. thaliana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Yamaguchi M, Mitsuda N, Ohtani M, Ohme-Takagi M, Kato K, Demura T. VASCULAR-RELATED NAC-DOMAIN 7 directly regulates the expression of a broad range of genes for xylem vessel formation. Plant J. 2011;66:57990." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2319">21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Zhong R, Ye Z-H. Complexity of the transcriptional network controlling secondary wall biosynthesis. Plant Sci. 2014;229:193207." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2323">49</a>]<i>.</i> To identify the <i>T. sinense</i> genes orthologous to these genes, we explored the evolutionary history of <i>VNS</i>, <i>LBD</i>, <i>R2R3-MYB</i>, <i>CesA</i>, and <i>PLCP</i> gene families.</p><p>The <i>VNS</i> gene family contains <i>VND</i>, <i>NST</i>, and <i>SMB</i> subfamilies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Ohtani M, Nishikubo N, Xu B, Yamaguchi M, Mitsuda N, Goue N, et al. A NAC domain protein family contributing to the regulation of wood formation in poplar. Plant J. 2011;67:499512." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2363">45</a>]. As shown in the phylogenetic tree, genes homologous to <i>VND</i>, <i>NST</i>, and <i>SMB</i> each fell into different clades with high bootstrap values and each clade did not contain sequences from <i>Physcomitrella patens</i> (also known as <i>Physcomitrium patens</i>) and <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i>, suggesting that the origin of these subfamilies occurred after the divergence of lycophytes. In the phylogenetic tree (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig4">4</a>a; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S11a), genes from the <i>VND</i> group clustered into two large clades: one including <i>A. thaliana VND4</i><i>VND6</i> and the other including <i>VND1</i><i>VND3</i> and <i>VND7</i>. Each of these clades contains sequences from gymnosperms (in black in the tree, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig4">4</a>a) and angiosperms, suggesting that the duplication leading to the ancestors of <i>VND4</i><i>VND6</i>, and <i>VND1</i><i>VND3</i> and <i>VND7</i> occurred before the divergence of gymnosperms and angiosperms. In the clade containing <i>VND1</i><i>VND3</i> and <i>VND7</i>, genes homologous to <i>VND1</i><i>VND3</i> and <i>VND7</i> each formed a subclade and each subclade only contained sequences from angiosperms, suggesting that the duplication leading to the origin of the <i>VND7</i> ancestor occurred after the divergence of angiosperms and gymnosperms. Sequences from the <i>VND7</i> clade were aligned and two diagnostic domains of <i>VND7</i> subfamily were found in all these sequences (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S11b), suggesting that they are bona fide <i>VND7</i> genes.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="989" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Regulatory relationships between TsVND7 and vessel-related genes in <i>T. sinense.</i> <b>a</b> Maximum likelihood tree of genes from the <i>VNS</i> gene family. Bootstrap percentages for the large clades are shown next to the nodes. Species name abbreviations given in Fig. S11 in additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>. Angiosperm sequences of different subfamilies are shown in different colors and the gymnosperm sequences are shown in black. <b>b</b> Confocal images of <i>Nicotiana benthamiana</i> leaves transiently transformed with <i>TsVND7.2-GFP</i>, <i>TsVND6.1-GFP</i>, and <i>TsVND6.2-GFP</i> under the control of the 35S promoter, bar=10m. <b>c</b> Yeast one-hybrid assay showing that <i>Ts</i>VND6.1 and <i>Ts</i>LBD30a interact with the <i>TsVND7.2</i> promotor and <i>Ts</i>VND7.2 interacts with the <i>TsCesA4</i>, <i>TsMYB</i>, and <i>TsXCPa</i> promoters. <b>d</b> Luciferase activity assay showing that <i>Ts</i>VND6.1 interacts with promoters of <i>TsVND7.1</i> and <i>TsVND7.2</i>; <i>Ts</i>LBD30a and <i>Ts</i>LBD30b interact with the <i>TsVND7.2</i> promoter; <i>Ts</i>VND7.1 interacts with the <i>TsCesA8</i> and <i>TsXCPa</i> promoters; and <i>Ts</i>VND7.2 interacts with the <i>TsMYB</i> and <i>TsCesA4</i> promoters. <b>e</b> Diagram showing the interaction relationship determined in <i>Arabidopsis</i>. <b>f</b> Diagram showing the interaction relationship from the results of Y1H and luciferase activity assay in <b>c</b> and <b>d</b> and supplementary Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">13</a> bd. The orange lines show the interactions evidenced by Y1H and luciferase activity assays, the green line shows the interactions evidenced by Y1H, and blue lines show the interactions evidenced by luciferase activity assay</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The transcription factors ASL19/LBD30 and ASL20/LBD18 are involved in a positive feedback loop to regulate <i>VND7</i> expression [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Soyano T, Thitamadee S, Machida Y, Chua NH. ASYMMETRIC LEAVES2-LIKE19/LATERAL ORGAN BOUNDARIES DOMAIN30 and ASL20/LBD18 regulate tracheary element differentiation in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell. 2008;20:335973." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2603">27</a>]. We identified 27 <i>LBD18/LBD30</i> genes in angiosperms. However, no <i>LBD18/LBD30</i> genes were identified in genomes of three gymnosperm species, one lycophyte, and one moss, suggesting that the origin of the <i>LBD18</i> subfamily may have occurred after the divergence of angiosperms and gymnosperms. In the ML tree (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S12a), the two genes (<i>AtLBD18</i> and <i>AtLBD30</i>) from <i>A. thaliana</i> formed one clade, suggesting that they originated from lineage-specific duplications. The two copies in <i>T. sinense</i> (Tesin01G0033700 and Tesin21G0059700) are putative orthologs to <i>A. thaliana AtLBD18</i> and <i>AtLBD30</i>.</p><p>In total, 87 <i>R2R3-MYB</i> genes were identified in the <i>T. sinense</i> genome. These sequences together with sequences from <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> were subjected to phylogenetic analysis. As shown in the ML tree (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S12b), sequences from the three species fell into different clades. All subgroups (except for 2 subgroups only including sequences from <i>A. thaliana</i>) contain sequences from <i>A. thaliana/T. sinense</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>, suggesting these subgroups originated before the splitting of monocots and dicots. In subgroup MYB46 and MYB83, there are three <i>T. sinense</i> sequences (Tesin13G0114100, Tesin11G0137400, Tesin07G144300).</p><p>In the ML tree (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S12c), the <i>CesA</i> sequences of <i>P. patens</i> formed their own clade. CesAs in seed plants fell into six major clades, which were designated as six subfamilies: <i>CesA1</i>/<i>10</i>, <i>CesA3</i>, <i>CesA6</i>, <i>CesA4</i>, <i>CesA7</i>, and <i>CesA8</i>. Each subfamily included sequences from angiosperms and gymnosperms, indicating the occurrence of ancient gene duplication events before the divergence of angiosperms and gymnosperms. In the phylogenetic tree, subfamilies <i>CesA4</i>, <i>CesA7</i>, and <i>CesA8</i> clustered together and the remaining subfamilies clustered together. <i>CesA</i> sequences of <i>S. moellendorffii</i> fell into each of these two clades. All subfamilies, including subfamilies <i>CesA4</i> and <i>CesA8</i>, also contained only one <i>T. sinense</i> gene except for subfamily <i>CesA1/10</i>, which contained three <i>T. sinense</i> genes.</p><p>Congruent with previous studies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Richau KH, Kaschani F, Verdoes M, Pansuriya TC, Niessen S, Stueber K, et al. Subclassification and biochemical analysis of plant papain-like cysteine proteases displays subfamily-specific characteristics.
