Plant Physiol. 2012;158:158399." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2739">48</a>], most <i>PLCP</i> genes fell into nine subfamilies (<i>SAG12</i>, <i>THI</i>, <i>CEP</i>, <i>XCP</i>, <i>RD21</i>, <i>XBCP3</i>, <i>RD19</i>, <i>ALP</i>, and <i>CTB</i>). SAG12, THI, CEP, XCP, RD21, and XBCP3 have been classified as L-like cathepsins, and RD19, ALP, and CTB have been classified as F, H, and B-like cathepsins [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Richau KH, Kaschani F, Verdoes M, Pansuriya TC, Niessen S, Stueber K, et al. Subclassification and biochemical analysis of plant papain-like cysteine proteases displays subfamily-specific characteristics. Plant Physiol. 2012;158:158399." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2774">48</a>], respectively. In the phylogenetic tree (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S12d, e), all subfamilies of L-like cathepsins clustered together while F, H, and B-like cathepsins were relatively distant from them. L, F, H, and B-like proteins appeared in the moss and lycophyte, suggesting that ancient duplications led to the origin of these subfamilies. As shown in the ML tree, <i>XCP</i> genes originated before the divergence of gymnosperms from <i>S. moellendorffii.</i></p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Nuclear localization and transcriptional regulatory network of <i>VND</i> genes</h3><p>To investigate the intrinsic function of VNDs in <i>T. sinense</i>, we first analyzed their subcellular localization by transient expression. Like <i>Arabidopsis VND6</i> and <i>VND7</i>, <i>Ts</i>VND7.2-GFP, <i>Ts</i>VND6.1-GFP, and <i>Ts</i>VND6.2-GFP predominantly localize to the nucleus, as seen by co-localization assays with GPF fluorescence merged with a bright-field image (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig4">4</a>b; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S13a).</p><p>Yeast one-hybrid (Y1H) analysis was subsequently performed to determine the interaction between <i>Ts</i>VND7.1 and <i>Ts</i>VND7.2 and other genes. The yeast-carrying Bait-BD-pHIS2 vectors expressing the promoter of <i>TsVND7.1</i>, <i>TsVND7.2</i>, <i>TsMYB</i>, <i>TsCesA4</i>, <i>TsCesA8</i>, <i>TsXCPa</i>, or <i>TsXCPc</i> grew normally on selective medium without 3-AT but failed to grow with 3-AT, showing that no self-activation occurred (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S13b). The following tests were carried out by promoters fused to Bait-BD-pHIS2 co-transformed with transcription factors fused to Prey-AD-pGADT7. The results showed that <i>Ts</i>VND7.2 interacted with the promoters of <i>TsMYB</i>, <i>TsCesA4</i> and <i>TsXCPa</i>, and <i>TsVND7.2</i> can be regulated by <i>Ts</i>VND6.1 and <i>Ts</i>LBD30a (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig4">4</a>c). Conversely, <i>Ts</i>VND7.1 did not interact with the <i>TsMYB</i>, <i>TsCesA4</i>, <i>TsCesA8</i>, <i>TsXCPa</i>, <i>TsXCPb</i>, or <i>TsXCPc</i> promoters and was not regulated by <i>Ts</i>LBD30a. <i>Ts</i>VND7.2 also did not bind to the promoters of <i>TsCesA8</i>, <i>TsXCPb</i>, and <i>TsXCPc</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S13c).</p><p>The interaction between <i>TsVND7.1/TsVND7.2</i> and other genes was further assessed using luciferase activity assays (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig4">4</a>d; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S13d). In these assays, <i>Ts</i>VND7.2 interacted with promoters of <i>TsMYB</i>, <i>TsCesA4</i>, <i>TsCesA8</i>, <i>TsXCPa</i> and <i>TsXCPc</i>, and <i>TsVND7.2</i> can be regulated by <i>Ts</i>VND6.1, <i>Ts</i>LBD30a, and <i>Ts</i>LBD30a. <i>TsVND7.1</i> also showed similar interactions with other genes except <i>TsLBD30a</i>, <i>TsCesA4</i>, and <i>TsXCPc</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Demographic history analysis of <i>T. sinense</i>
</h3><p>To explore the historical demographic fluctuations and present-day genetic diversity within this endemic species, we resequenced 55 individuals of <i>T. sinense</i> from a range-wide sampling from China (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig5">5</a>a). ADMIXTURE analysis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Alexander DH, Novembre J, Lange K. Fast model-based estimation of ancestry in unrelated individuals. Genome Res. 2009;19:165564." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d33374e2997">50</a>] of Chinese samples revealed that the deepest splits occurred among the southwestern population (population YN), the populations near the Sichuan Basin in central China (represented by populations CQ from the Jinfo Mountains and SC from the Emei Mountains), and the populations from the North and East parts of central China (represented by populations SNJ from the Shennong Mountains and populations SX and TS from the Qingling Mountains) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig5">5</a>b; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S14). When <i>K</i>=4, the TS population from the Qinling Mountains in north-central China diverged from the third clusters and four ancestries of <i>T. sinense</i> occurred in China (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig5">5</a>b). Principal component analysis (PCA) and phylogenetic analysis also revealed four major clusters (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig5">5</a>c, d; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S14b, c).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="810" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Phylogenetic relationships and population structure of <i>Tetracentron</i> populations. <b>a</b> Geographic distribution of the sampling locations. <b>b</b> Results of STRUCTURE analysis, showing four significant genetic clusters. <b>c</b> Principal component analysis (PCA), with the proportion of the variance explained being 11.3% for PC1 and 5.20% for PC2. <b>d</b> A neighbor-joining (NJ) phylogenetic tree. e Correlation between <i>F</i>st and geographic distance by Mantel test (<i>r</i>=0.8417, <i>p</i>=0.004). f Detection of gene flow between <i>Tetracentron</i> populations. Lines with arrows indicates gene flow and direction of gene flow. The color scale: red indicates strong gene flow and yellow indicates weak gene flow. The horizontal scale bar at the bottom (drift parameter) shows a tenfold average standard error of the entries in the sample covariance matrix</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>T. sinense</i> exhibited higher within-species genetic diversity than within-population level genetic diversity. The within-population genetic diversity of <i>T. sinense</i> (6.3011.58e3 for Wattersons estimator (<sub>w</sub>) and 6.4811.25e3 for nucleotide diversity (), Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S3) was obviously low compared to the species level diversity. The genetic differentiation statistics (fixation index; <i>F</i>st) further showed that genetic differentiation between YN population and all other populations (0.2930.382) was higher than those between others (most &lt;0.293), and the genetic differentiation between SX and SNJ population (0.102) was the lowest (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S4).</p><p>The Mantel test revealed a significant positive correlation between the genetic and geographic distance (<i>r</i>=0.8417, <i>p</i>=0.004, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig5">5</a>e). To estimate gene flow between populations, we performed Treemix v1.13. analysis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Pickrell JK, Pritchard JK. Inference of population splits and mixtures from genome-wide allele frequency data. PLoS Genet. 2012;8:e1002967." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3099">51</a>], which suggested that the TS population from the Qinling Mountains in north-central China is the result of admixture between the SC population the near Sichuan Basin and the SX population from the Qinling Mountains in the north and east parts of central China (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig5">5</a>f). Gene flow is also observed in population SC and CQ. It is consistent with that they were in the same group according to phylogenetic, PCA, and structure analysis.</p><p>An analysis of demographic history using stairway plots revealed bottlenecks the populations may have undergone (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig6">6</a>), including one ranging 0.30.67 MYA, and a recent bottleneck around 0.03 MYA. For three populations (SC, SX, and YN), their effective population size is declining since they were affected by the Last Glacial Maximum and their extinction is underway.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02198-7/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2198_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="306" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Demographic history of <i>T. sinense</i>. Stairway plot showing historical changes in effective population size (<i>y</i>-axis) for six populations (CQ, SC, SNJ, SX, TS, YN) with a generation time of 15years. Gray shadows highlight bottlenecks</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Polyploidization in the early evolution of eudicots</h3><p>Previous studies suggested that Ranunculales, Proteales, Trochodendrales, and Buxales formed the grade of early diverging eudicots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Soltis DE, Smith SA, Cellinese N, Wurdack KJ, Tank DC, Brockington SF, et al. Angiosperm phylogeny: 17 genes, 640 taxa. Am J Bot. 2011;98:70430." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3148">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chase MW, Soltis DE, Olmstead RG, Morgan D. Phylogenetics of seed plants: an analysis of nucleotide sequences from the plastid gene rbcL. Ann Mo Bot Gard. 1993;80:52880." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3151">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Soltis DE, Soltis PS, Nickrent DL, Johnson LA, Hahn WJ, Hoot SB, et al. Angiosperm phylogeny inferred from 18s ribosomal DNA sequences. Ann Mo Bot Gard. 1997;84:149." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3154">11</a>]. However, these analyses mainly relied on chloroplast and mitochondrial genes as well as a few nuclear ribosomal genes. It is particularly necessary to compare organellar gene-based phylogenetic estimates with topologies derived from analyses of low-copy nuclear genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Zeng L, Zhang Q, Sun R, Kong H, Zhang N, Ma H. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat Commun. 2014;5:4956." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3157">1</a>]. The <i>T. sinense</i> genome provides an opportunity to resolve the relationships between lineages based on genome-wide low-copy nuclear genes. Our analysis based on 214 single-copy nuclear gene families (orthogroups) provides strong support for the conclusion that the Ranunculales, Proteales, and Trochodendrales are as successive sisters to the core eudicots. Within eudicots, the Ranunculales diverged at ~136 (129140) MYA, followed by the Proteales ~131 (123137) MYA, the Trochodendrales ~125 (117132) MYA, and the core eudicots ~113 (105123) MYA (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig1">1</a>). This estimate is generally consistent with several estimates from other relaxed molecular clock analyses based on DNA and/or genomic data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Li HT, Yi TS, Gao ML, Ma PF, Zhang T, Yang JB, et al. Origin of angiosperms and the puzzle of the jurassic gap. Nat Plants. 2019;5:46170." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3167">36</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Strijk JS, Hinsinger DD, Zhang F, Cao K. Trochodendron aralioides, the first chromosome-level draft genome in Trochodendrales and a valuable resource for basal eudicot research. Gigascience. 2019;8: &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/gigascience/giz136&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3170">52</a>]. The narrow range of ages suggests a rapid diversification of the early eudicots. In Trochodendrales, the divergence time of <i>Tetracentron</i><i>Trochodendron</i> is approximately 31 MYA (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig1">1</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S3). This is generally congruent with the age of the earliest reliable fossils of <i>Tetracentron</i> and <i>Trochodendron</i>, which date to the early middle Eocene epoch [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Pigg KB, Wehr WC, Ickert-Bond SM. Trochodendron and nordenskioldia (Trochodendraceae) from the middle eocene of Washington State, USA. Int J Plant Sci. 2011;162:118798." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3192">30</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Pigg KB, Dillhoff RM, DeVore ML, Wehr WC. New diversity among the Trochodendraceae from the early/middle eocene Okanogan highlands of British Columbia, Canada, and northeastern Washington State, United States. Int J Plant Sci. 2007;168:52132." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3195">53</a>].</p><p>Prevalent polyploidization events have been identified during the evolutionary history of angiosperms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wu S, Han B, Jiao Y. Genetic contribution of paleopolyploidy to adaptive evolution in angiosperms. Mol Plant. 2020;13:5971." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3201">54</a>]. Previous studies demonstrated that the rise and eventual dominance of core eudicots might have benefited from the  hexaploidization event in the most recent common ancestor of core eudicots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Jiao Y, Leebens-Mack J, Ayyampalayam S, Bowers JE, Mckain MR, McNeal J, et al. A genome triplication associated with early diversification of the core eudicots. Genome Biol. 2012;13:R3." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3204">4</a>]. Based on genomic investigations of <i>V. vinifera</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Lyons E, Pedersen B, Kane J, Freeling M. The value of nonmodel genomes and an expample using synmap within CoGe to dissect the hexaploidy that predates the rosids. Tropical Plant Biol. 2008;1:18190." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3210">55</a>] and <i>N. nucifera</i>, Ming et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ming R, Leebens-Mack J, Ayyampalayam S, Bowers JE, McKain MR, McNeal J, et al. Genome of the long-living sacred lotus (Nelumbo nucifera Gaertn.). Genome Biol. 2013;14:R41." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3217">13</a>] proposed that the triplication event involved an initial tetraploidization event after the divergence of early diverging eudicots, then involved hybridization with a now-extinct species that branched off around the same time as, or earlier than <i>N. nucifera</i>. By contrast, a recent study using the <i>A. coerulea</i> genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Akoez G, Nordborg M. The Aquilegia genome reveals a hybrid origin of core eudicots. Genome Biol. 2019;20:256." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3226">5</a>] also proposed a hybrid origin of core eudicots, involving a tetraploidization event that occurred in the ancestor of all eudicots, then a hexaploidization in the ancestor of core eudicots. In this study, we performed comprehensive analyses including synteny, <i>Ks</i>, and phylogenomic analysis to clarify the nature of the  event. Our results strongly suggested that the WGDs that occurred in <i>A. coerulea</i>, <i>N. nucifera</i>, and the two species of Trochodendrales are lineage-specific, and no WGD occurred in the common ancestor of all eudicots (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig2">2</a>g). The ancient WGD () in Trochodendrales dates to about 5954 MYA, which coincides with the CretaceousTertiary (KT) mass extinction event [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Fawcett JA, Maere S, Van de Peer Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the cretaceous-tertiary extinction event. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2009;106:573742." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3242">56</a>]. In fact, many independent WGDs have been identified in many lineages and have been proposed to have contributed to survival during this period of dramatic environmental changes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wu S, Han B, Jiao Y. Genetic contribution of paleopolyploidy to adaptive evolution in angiosperms. Mol Plant. 2020;13:5971." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3245">54</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Fawcett JA, Maere S, Van de Peer Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the cretaceous-tertiary extinction event. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2009;106:573742." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3248">56</a>].</p><p>It is also worth noting that after the two successive WGDs in <i>T. sinense</i>, the genome structure remained well conserved. <i>Ks</i> analyses for orthologs between <i>T. sinense/T. aralioides</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> showed the Trochodendrales lineage has the lowest nucleotide substitution rate among the studied species (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig2">2</a>e)<i>.</i> The lineage nucleotide substitution rate in <i>N. nucifera</i> is about 30% lower than that of <i>V. vinifera</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ming R, Leebens-Mack J, Ayyampalayam S, Bowers JE, McKain MR, McNeal J, et al. Genome of the long-living sacred lotus (Nelumbo nucifera Gaertn.). Genome Biol. 2013;14:R41." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3279">13</a>]. We found that the nucleotide substitution rate of <i>T. sinense</i> is much lower than <i>N. nucifera</i>, since the peak of the <i>Ks</i> distribution of orthologs between <i>T. sinense</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i> is smaller than that of <i>N. nucifera</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i> (representing the same divergence event) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig2">2</a>e). Together, these results indicate that the genome of <i>T. sinense</i> could serve as an exceptional comparative reference genome for investigating early evolution of eudicots.</p><p>Genomic analysis of gene families can shed light on the morphological characteristics shared by different clades. The early diverging lineages of eudicots tend to show some ancestral characteristics that differentiate them from the core eudicots. For example, the flowers of core eudicots are organized in a predictable manner with a stable number of parts (e.g., flowers with parts in five or multiples of five, a clear differentiation of sepals and petals), but in the Trochodendrales (like in some early angiosperms), the perianth is not differentiated into typical sepals and petals [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Endress PK. Floral structure, systematics, and phylogeny in Trochodendrales. Ann Mo Bot Gard. 1986;73:297324." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3314">8</a>]. MADS-box (ABCE) genes are among the most important regulators of flower development. Analysis of the <i>Amborella</i> genome revealed that duplication and diversification of floral MADS-box genes likely occurred before the origin of extant angiosperms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Albert VA, Barbazuk WB, de Pamphilis CW, Der JP L-MJ, Ma H, et al. The amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science. 2013;342:1241089." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3320">40</a>]. Our results showed that A-, B-, C-, E-function genes did not significantly expand or contract in the lineages leading to eudicots and core eudicots. The differences in flower structure between eudicots and core eudicots may be due to the differences between broadly overlapping ABCE gene expression patterns in early diverging eudicots and angiosperms, and strict spatially restricted ABCE gene expression in core eudicots, as suggested by some studies in Nymphaeales, Magnoliids, and Ranunculales [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zhang L, Chen F, Zhang X, Li Z, Zhao Y, Lohaus R, et al. The water lily genome and the early evolution of flowering plants. Nature. 2020;577:7984." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3323">41</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Chanderbali AS, Yoo M-J, Zahn LM, Brockington SF, Wall PK, Gitzendanner MA, et al. Conservation and canalization of gene expression during angiosperm diversification accompany the origin and evolution of the flower. Proc Natl Acad Sci 2010;107:2257022575." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3326">57</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Sharma B, Kramer EM. Aquilegia B gene homologs promote petaloidy of the sepals and maintenance of the C domain boundary. Evodevo. 2017;8:22." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3330">58</a>]. Notably, in the lineage leading to core eudicots, the TM3 MADS-box gene family that includes the important flowering time gene <i>SOC1</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Lee H, Suh SS, Park E, Cho E, Ahn JH, Kim SG, et al. The AGAMOUS-LIKE 20 MADS domain protein integrates floral inductive pathways in Arabidopsis. Gen dev. 2000;14:236676." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3336">59</a>] has expanded. In the genome of <i>T. sinense</i>, the <i>SVP</i> gene family also expanded rapidly. This family includes the MAD-box gene <i>AGL24</i>, which is not only involved in the flowering transition through activation of <i>SOC1</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Lee H, Suh SS, Park E, Cho E, Ahn JH, Kim SG, et al. The AGAMOUS-LIKE 20 MADS domain protein integrates floral inductive pathways in Arabidopsis. Gen dev. 2000;14:236676." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3352">59</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Liu C, Chen H, Er HL, Soo HM, Kumar PP, Han J-H, et al. Direct interaction of AGL24 and SOC1 integrates flowering signals in Arabidopsis. Development. 2008;135:148191." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3355">60</a>] but also in flower development together with class A and E genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Gregis V, Sessa A, Colombo L, Kater MM. AGAMOUS-LIKE24 and SHORT VEGETATIVE PHASE determine floral meristem identity in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 2008;56:891902." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3358">61</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="de Folter S, Immink RGH, Kieffer M, Parenicova L, Henz SR, Weigel D, et al. Comprehensive interaction map of the Arabidopsis MADS box transcription factors. Plant Cell. 2005;17:142433." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3361">62</a>]. The expanded <i>SVP</i> clades could be involved in the complex networks regulating flowering in <i>T. sinense</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Vessel elements in secondary xylem of <i>T. sinense</i> and the transcriptional regulation of <i>VND7</i>-related genes</h3><p>The presence of vessels in the xylem of angiosperms and their absence in the xylem of gymnosperms (except for gnetophytes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wan T, Liu Z-M, Li L-F, Leitch AR, Leitch IJ, Lohaus R, et al. A genome for gnetophytes and early evolution of seed plants. Nat Plants. 2018;4(2):829." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3386">15</a>]) is one of the striking differences between these two large groups of plants. The Tetracentraceae (also as the Trochodendraceae), however, is one of several angiosperm families that have been described as lacking vessels. Carlquist [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Carlquist S, Schneider EL. The tracheid-vessel element transition in angiosperms involves multiple independent features: cladistic consequences. Am J Bot. 2002;89:18595." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3389">63</a>] found porous pit membranes on the end walls of early wood tracheary elements and considered that tracheary elements in <i>T. sinense</i> are transitional. According to the study of Ren et al., vessel elements exist in <i>T. sinense</i>, but they are of a primitive type [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ren Y, Chen L, Tian XH, Zhang XH, Lu AM. Discovery of vessels in Tetracentron (Trochodendraceae) and its systematic significance. Plant Syst Evol. 2007;267:15561." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3398">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li H-F, Chaw S-M, Du C-M, Ren Y. Vessel elements present in the secondary xylem of Trochodendron and Tetracentron (Trochodendraceae). Flora. 2011;206(6):595600." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3402">20</a>]. Our light microscopy and SEM observations of slices of <i>T. sinense</i> wood and analyses of X-ray computed microtomography (microCT) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig3">3</a>) provide strong evidence that there are two types of tracheary elements and some of the peculiar fusiform tracheary elements with obvious end-wall perforation plates in <i>T. sinense</i> are vessels.</p><p><i>VND7</i> belongs to the <i>VND</i> subfamily of the <i>VNS</i> gene family and is thought to be the master regulator of vessel differentiation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Yamaguchi M, Mitsuda N, Ohtani M, Ohme-Takagi M, Kato K, Demura T. VASCULAR-RELATED NAC-DOMAIN 7 directly regulates the expression of a broad range of genes for xylem vessel formation. Plant J. 2011;66:57990." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3425">21</a>], as <i>VND7</i> can induce various cells to trans-differentiate into vessel elements in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and other species. Gene family analyses showed that <i>VND7</i> occurred in angiosperms including in <i>T. sinense</i> and <i>T. aralioides</i>, but not in gymnosperms, indicating that the duplication event leading to the origin of the <i>VND7</i> ancestor occurred after the divergence of angiosperms and gymnosperms and before the split of angiosperm lineages (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig4">4</a>a; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S11a). This is consistent with a previous study that found no orthologs of <i>VND7</i> or <i>VND13</i> in gymnosperms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wan T, Liu Z-M, Li L-F, Leitch AR, Leitch IJ, Lohaus R, et al. A genome for gnetophytes and early evolution of seed plants. Nat Plants. 2018;4(2):829." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3460">15</a>]. The gnetophyte <i>Gnetum montanum</i> contains vessel-like water-conducting cells. However, the lack of <i>VND7</i> in <i>G. montanum</i> suggested that different molecular mechanisms underpin the origin and development of vessels in <i>Gnetum</i> and angiosperms, and the vessels in <i>Gnetum</i> and angiosperms are convergent characters [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wan T, Liu Z-M, Li L-F, Leitch AR, Leitch IJ, Lohaus R, et al. A genome for gnetophytes and early evolution of seed plants. Nat Plants. 2018;4(2):829." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3479">15</a>]. By contrast, based on the phylogenetic analysis of the <i>VNS</i> gene family, we identified <i>TsVND7.1</i> and <i>TsVND7.2</i> of <i>T. sinense</i> as orthologs of <i>A. thaliana VND7</i>. Multiple sequence alignments showed that TsVND7.1 and TsVND7.2 have the same seven conserved subdomains as other VNDs and the conserved sites important for the functions of VNDs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S11b). A transient expression assay showed that VND-GFP fluorescence localized to the nuclei of plant cells (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig4">4</a>b; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S13a) implying that VNDs in <i>T. sinense</i> act as nuclear transcription factors, as in <i>A. thaliana</i>.</p><p>Xylem vessel formation is regulated by a complex transcriptional network. <i>VND7</i> transcription factors directly regulate many genes including <i>MYB46</i>/<i>MYB83</i>, <i>CESA4</i> and <i>CESA8</i>, and <i>XCP1</i>/<i>XCP2</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yamaguchi M, Mitsuda N, Ohtani M, Ohme-Takagi M, Kato K, Demura T. VASCULAR-RELATED NAC-DOMAIN 7 directly regulates the expression of a broad range of genes for xylem vessel formation. Plant J. 2011;66:57990." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3539">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhong R, Richardson EA, Ye ZH. The MYB46 transcription factor is a direct target of SND1 and regulates secondary wall biosynthesis in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell. 2007;19:277692." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3539_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="McCarthy RL, Zhong R, Ye ZH. MYB83 is a direct target of SND1 and acts redundantly with MYB46 in the regulation of secondary cell wall biosynthesis in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell Physiol. 2009;50:195064." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3539_2">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim WC, Ko JH, Kim JY, Kim J, Bae HJ, Han KH. MYB46 directly regulates the gene expression of secondary wall-associated cellulose synthases in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 2013;73:2636." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3539_3">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Somerville C. Cellulose synthesis in higher plants. Annu Rev Cell Dev Biol. 2006;22:5378." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3539_4">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Funk V, Kositsup B, Zhao C, Beers EP. The Arabidopsis xylem peptidase XCP1 is a tracheary element vacuolar protein that may be a papain ortholog. Plant Physiol. 2002;128:8494." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3542">26</a>]<i>.</i> These genes are involved in vessel element differentiation, specifically in secondary cell wall formation and PCD. <i>VND7</i> is also positively regulated by several genes including <i>LBD18/LBD30</i> and <i>VND1</i><i>VND5</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ohashi-Ito K, Iwamoto K, Fukuda H. LOB domaincontaining protein 15 positively regulates expression of VND7, a master regulator of tracheary elements. Plant Cell Physiol. 2018;59:98996." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3561">14</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Soyano T, Thitamadee S, Machida Y, Chua NH. ASYMMETRIC LEAVES2-LIKE19/LATERAL ORGAN BOUNDARIES DOMAIN30 and ASL20/LBD18 regulate tracheary element differentiation in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell. 2008;20:335973." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3564">27</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Endo H, Yamaguchi M, Tamura T, Nakano Y, Nishikubo N, Yoneda A, et al. Multiple classes of transcription factors regulate the expression of VASCULAR-RELATED NAC-DOMAIN7, a master switch of xylem vessel differentiation. Plant Cell Physiol. 2015;56:24254." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3567">28</a>]. Gene family analyses suggested that all subfamilies that these genes belong to originated before the divergence of the angiosperms. We found that two genes, <i>TsLBD30a</i> (Tesin01G0033700) and <i>TsLBD3b</i> (Tesin21G0059700), are orthologs of the <i>AtLBD18</i>/<i>30</i> gene pair; three <i>TsMYBs</i> (Tesin13G0114100, Tesin11G0137400 and Tesin07G0144300) were found to be orthologous to <i>MYB46</i>/<i>MYB83</i>; <i>TsCesA4</i> (Tesin16G0055000) and <i>TsCesA8</i> (Tesin01G0177100) are orthologs of <i>AtCesA4</i> and <i>AtCesA8</i>, respectively; and <i>TsXCPa/b/c</i> (Tesin02G0006000, Tesin24G0110100, and Tesin06G0166500) are orthologous to <i>AtXCP1</i> and <i>AtXCP2</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Supplementary Notes and Fig. S12). To ascertain whether these genes can regulate or are regulated by TsVND7.1 and TsVND7.2, we performed yeast one-hybrid analysis and luciferase activity assay. Yeast one-hybrid analysis showed that TsVND6.1 and TsLBD30a proteins can bind to the <i>TsVND7</i>.2 promoter and TsVND7.2 can interact with the promoters of <i>TsMYB</i>, <i>TsCESA4</i>, and <i>TsXCPa</i>. Luciferase activity assays not only confirmed the interactions between <i>TsVND7.2</i> and relevant genes as shown by yeast one-hybrid, but also identified the interaction between TsVND7.2 and <i>TsLAB30b</i>/<i>TsCesA8</i> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig4">4</a>e). Although the two techniques showed some differences, the main results of both techniques demonstrated that the regulatory relationships between <i>TsVND7</i> and vessel-related genes are similar to those in <i>A. thaliana</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig4">4</a>f), supporting the anatomical observations that vessel elements are present in <i>T. sinense</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Demographic history of <i>T. sinense</i>
</h3><p><i>T. sinense</i> was widespread in many parts of the Northern Hemisphere until the Pliocene epoch of the Quarternary period [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Pigg KB, Wehr WC, Ickert-Bond SM. Trochodendron and nordenskioldia (Trochodendraceae) from the middle eocene of Washington State, USA. Int J Plant Sci. 2011;162:118798." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3670">30</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ozaki K. Tetracentron leaves from the neogene of Japan. Trans Proc Palaeont Soc Japan N. S. 1987;146:7787." href="#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3673">64</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Suzuki M, Joshi L, Noshiro S. Tetracentron wood from the Miocene of Noto Peninsula, Central Japan, with a short revision of homoxylic fossil woods. Bot Mag Tokyo. 1991;104:3748." href="#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3673_1">65</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Manchester SR, Crane PR, Dilcher DL. Nordenskioldia and Trochodendron (Trochodendraceae) from the Miocene of Northwestern North America. Botanical Gaz. 1991;152:35768." href="#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3673_2">66</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Grimsson F, Denk T, Zetter R. Pollen, fruits, and leaves of Tetracentron (Trochodendraceae) from the Cainozoic of Iceland and Western North America and their palaeobiogeographic implications. Grana. 2008;47:114." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3676">67</a>], indicating that the present-day endemism of <i>T. sinense</i> in east Asian is refugial. Previous studies identified five or three refugia in China [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sun Y, Moore MJ, Yue L, Feng T, Chu H, Chen S, et al. Chloroplast phylogeography of the East Asian Arcto-Tertiary relict Tetracentron sinense (Trochodendraceae). J Biogeogr. 2014;41:172132." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3682">31</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Li S, Gan X, Han H, Zhang X, Tian Z. Low within-population genetic diversity and high genetic differentiation among populations of the endangered plant Tetracentron sinense Oliver revealed by inter-simple sequence repeat analysis. Ann For Sci. 2018;75:74." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3686">32</a>]. Our ADMIXTURE analysis of whole genomic SNPs of Chinese samples revealed four major groups (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig5">5</a>b; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S14). In good agreement with the ADMIXTURE analysis, principal component analysis (PCA) and phylogenetic analysis also revealed the same four major clusters (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig5">5</a>c, d; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S14). Hence, our results suggested that in addition to the Southwestern China refuge [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sun Y, Moore MJ, Yue L, Feng T, Chu H, Chen S, et al. Chloroplast phylogeography of the East Asian Arcto-Tertiary relict Tetracentron sinense (Trochodendraceae). J Biogeogr. 2014;41:172132." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3701">31</a>], there were three refugia in central China: one in the Sichuan Basin corresponding to the area covered by the Jinfo and Emei Mountains, one in the east part including the Shennong Mountains, and one in the Qinling Mountains. These areas have long been regarded as important global Pleistocene glacial refugia and the areas around the Sichuan Basin of central China and the Shennong Mountains in the east of central China were also likely refugia for many other relict tree species such as <i>Cathaya</i>, <i>Cercidiphyllum</i>, <i>Ginkgo</i>, and <i>Taxus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tang CQ, Yang Y, Ohsawa M, Yi SR, Momohara A, Su WH, et al. Evidence for the persistence of wild Ginkgo biloba (ginkgoaceae) populations in the DALOU Moutains, southwestern China. Am J Bot. 2012;99:140814." href="#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3717">68</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhao YP, Fan G, Yin PP, Sun S, Li N, Hong X, Hu G, et al. Resequencing 545 Ginkgo genomes across the world reveals the evolutionary history of the living fossil. Nat Commun. 2019;10:4201." href="#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3717_1">69</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Qi XS, Chen C, Comes HP, Sakaguchi S, Liu YH, Tanaka N, et al. Molecular data and ecological niche modelling reveal a highly dynamic evolutionary history of the East Asian Tertiary relict Cercidiphyllum (Cercidiphyllaceae). New Phytol. 2012;196:61730." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3720">70</a>].</p><p>The genetic diversity of species is the result of historical evolution and also the basis of evolution; moreover, sufficient genetic variability is required for endangered species to survive and adapt to changing environments. Nucleotide diversity analysis of <i>T. sinense</i> showed high within-species genetic diversity (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S3) and its genetic diversity is higher than those reported for the relict species <i>A. trichopoda</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Albert VA, Barbazuk WB, de Pamphilis CW, Der JP L-MJ, Ma H, et al. The amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science. 2013;342:1241089." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3735">40</a>] and <i>Ginkgo biloba</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Zhao YP, Fan G, Yin PP, Sun S, Li N, Hong X, Hu G, et al. Resequencing 545 Ginkgo genomes across the world reveals the evolutionary history of the living fossil. Nat Commun. 2019;10:4201." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3742">69</a>]<i>.</i> The high genetic diversity detected in this tertiary relict species might reflect the accumulation of mutations over long evolutionary time scales and in populations experiencing geographical isolation and heterogeneous habitats. This high level of genetic diversity indicated that this endangered species maintains a relatively high evolutionary potential. Congruent with the previous study [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Li S, Gan X, Han H, Zhang X, Tian Z. Low within-population genetic diversity and high genetic differentiation among populations of the endangered plant Tetracentron sinense Oliver revealed by inter-simple sequence repeat analysis. Ann For Sci. 2018;75:74." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3748">32</a>], we observed a low genetic diversity within populations and high genetic differentiation among populations of <i>T. sinense</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S4). The small population effect and geographical isolation were proposed to the main factors [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Li S, Gan X, Han H, Zhang X, Tian Z. Low within-population genetic diversity and high genetic differentiation among populations of the endangered plant Tetracentron sinense Oliver revealed by inter-simple sequence repeat analysis. Ann For Sci. 2018;75:74." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3757">32</a>]. A population graph inferred by Treemix indicated similar relationships between populations to those revealed in the NJ tree and low levels of gene flow except between population SC and TS (0.4). Gene flow was fewer between distant populations, as confirmed by the Mantel test between the genetic and geographic distance (<i>r</i>=0.8417, <i>p</i>=0.004).</p><p>An analysis of demographic history using the stairway plot method revealed that all populations have undergone multiple cycles of expansion and contraction. During the early Quaternary, all populations underwent contraction, followed by expansion. For all populations, two significant bottlenecks were identified (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#Fig6">6</a>). The time of the more ancient bottleneck identified for two populations (CQ and SC) corresponds to the Naynayxungla glaciation (0.50.72 MYA, the most extensive glaciation in China [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Zheng B, Xu Q, Shen Y. The relationship between climate change and quaternary glacial cycles on the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau: review and speculation. Quat Int. 2002;97:93101." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3773">71</a>]) and the time of the more ancient bottleneck of the other four population corresponds to the Guixiang glaciation (0.130.3 MYA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Zheng B, Xu Q, Shen Y. The relationship between climate change and quaternary glacial cycles on the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau: review and speculation. Quat Int. 2002;97:93101." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3776">71</a>]). The most recent bottleneck of all six populations occurred around 0.03 MYA, corresponding to the Last Glacial Maximum (0.0010.007 MYA). For three populations (SC, SX, and YN), their effective population size is declining since they were affected by the Last Glacial Maximum and their extinction is likely underway. As ongoing effects of an expanding human population threaten <i>T. sinense</i> populations, conservation efforts should focus on preserving the genetic diversity of endemic <i>T. sinense</i> trees.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><p><i>Tetracentron sinense</i> belongs to the angiosperm order Trochodendrales, which consists of only one family (Trochodendraceae) and two species (<i>Tetracentron sinense</i> and <i>Trochodendron aralioides</i>). Based on the <i>Tetracentron sinense</i> reference genome and the previously published genomes from other species, our phylogenomic analyses support that the Trochodendrales is the sister lineage to the core eudicots. The genomes of the two species of Trochodendrales also revealed two WGD events ( and ) in Trochodendrales at approximately 82 and 59 MYA. Based on our comprehensive analysis including syntenic, <i>Ks</i> distribution and phylogenomic analysis, we conclude that the Trochodendrales did not experience the  event and the event may have only happened in core eudicots. Through light microscopy and SEM of slices of <i>T. sinense</i> wood and microCT, we found that vessel elements are present in <i>T. sinense.</i> Consistent with the anatomical results, the <i>T. sinense</i> genome contains two putative orthologs of <i>VND7</i>, a master regulator of angiosperm vessel formation, which appears to function in a similar transcriptional network in <i>T. sinense</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Population genomics analysis of individuals across the native range of <i>T. sinense</i> in China revealed that all the populations have undergone two bottlenecks and there is a high level of genetic diversity in the species. As <i>Tetracentron sinense</i> is a basal eudicot with unique tracheary elements, its genome provides a unique reference for inferring the early evolution of the eudicots and the mechanisms underlying the formation of vessel elements. The population genomics analysis facilitates the interpretation of their genetic diversity and geographic structure, thus providing genetic implications for future conservation.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec16" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec16-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec16">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec16-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Illumina short-read sequencing</h3><p>The sequenced individual of <i>Tetracentron sinense</i> is growing in the Kunming Botanical Garden, Kunming, China. Genomic DNA was extracted from fresh leaves and purified using the Tiangen Isolation/Extraction/Purification Kit (Tiangen Biotech (Beijing) Co., Ltd.). Then, it was fragmented with a Covaris M220 Focused-ultrasonicator Instrument. Illumina PCR-free libraries with insert sizes of 300500bp were constructed using the NEBNext Ultra DNA library Pre Kit for Illumina sequencing. Then, 150-bp paired-end sequencing was performed using the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome survey</h3><p>Using Illumina short reads, <i>k</i>-mer distribution was estimated using JELLYFISH v2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Marcais G, Kingsford CA. Fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:76470." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3860">72</a>]. The overall characteristics of the genome such as genome size, repeat contents, and heterozygosity rate were estimated using GCE software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Liu B, Shi Y, Yuan Y, Hu X, Zhang H, Li N, et. al. Estimation of genomic characteristics by analyzing k-mer frequency in de novo genome projects. arXiv. 2012:arXiv:1308.2012v2." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3863">73</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">PacBio library construction and sequencing</h3><p>SMRTbell libraries were constructed according to the manufacturers protocol of SMRTbell Express Template Preparation Kit (Pacific Biosciences). Briefly, high-quality and high-molecular-weight genomic DNA was purified using the Mobio PowerClean Pro DNA Clean-Up Kit, and DNA quality was assessed by agarose gel electrophoresis and Thermo Fisher Scientific Qubit Fluorometry. Genomic DNA was further sheared to a size range of 1550kb using AMPure beads (Beckman Coulte) or g-TUBE (Covaris) and enzymatically repaired and converted into SMRTbell template libraries. Then, hairpin adapters were ligated after exonuclease digestion. The resulting SMRTbell templates were size-selected by Blue Pippin electrophoresis (Sage Sciences). The size-selected SMRTbell DNA fragments ranging from 15 to 50kb were sequenced on a PacBio Sequel instrument using S/P2-C2 sequencing chemistry (21 cells, 14.7 million reads, ~100).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Hi-C library construction and sequencing</h3><p>Hi-C was performed according to the following procedure. Young leaves were fixed with formaldehyde and lysed, and then the cross-linked DNA was digested with the HindIII restriction enzyme and the 5 overhangs were biotinylated. After labeling with biotin-14-dCTP, the resulting free blunt ends were ligated again. Then, the purified DNA was treated to remove biotin from non-ligated DNA ends. For the fragmentation, DNA was then sheared with a Covaris M220 Focused-ultrasonicator Instrument. The sheared DNA was then repaired and biotin-containing fragments were isolated using streptavidin beads. A-tailing and adapters were ligated subsequently, and sequencing libraries were generated and sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq X Ten instrument with 150-bp paired-end reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Genome assembly and pseudomolecule building</h3><p>The primary assembly was performed with PacBio long reads using six different approaches, CANU (v0.1) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Koren S, Walenz BP, Berlin K, Miller JR, Bergman NH, Phillippy AM. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 2017;27:72236." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3891">74</a>], SMARTdenovo (v 0.2) (<a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo">https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo</a>), WTDBG (v0.3) (<a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg">https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg</a>), SMARTdenovo after CANU correction (v0.4), and WTDBG2 after CANU correction (v0.6). Then, based on the assembled genome size, number of contigs, average contig size, and N50, assemblies from SMARTdenovo (v 0.2), and WTDBG2 after CANU correction (v0.6) were selected for merging using quickmerge [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Chakraborty M, Baldwin-Brown JG, Long AD, Emerson JJ. Contiguous and accurate de novo assembly of metazoan genomes with modest long read coverage. Nucleic Acids Res. 2016;44:e147." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3908">75</a>] to improve the contiguity. Finally, the draft assembly was polished using PacBio long reads with Arrow software (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus</a>) and corrected using Illumina paired-end reads with Pilon software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Walker B, Abeel T, Shea T, Priest M, Abouelliel A, Sakthikumar S, et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One. 2014;9:e112963." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3919">76</a>].</p><p>Hi-C read pairs were aligned to the draft assembly v1.0 using Juicer software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Durand NC, Shamim MS, Machol I, Rao SS, Huntley MH, Lander ES, et al. Juicer provides a one-click system for analyzing loop-resolution Hi-C experiments. Cell Syst. 2016;3:958." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3925">77</a>]. The resulting contact matrices and draft assembly v1.0 were used for further Hi-C scaffolding with the 3D (3d-dna) pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Dudchenko O, Batra SS, Omer AD, Nyquist SK, Hoeger M, Durand NC, et al. De novo assembly of the aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science. 2017;356:925." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3928">78</a>]. This procedure includes correcting the mis-joins, scaffolding the input contigs, polishing the megascaffold, splitting the megascaffold into raw chromosomal scaffolds, and then sealing raw chromosomal scaffolds to yield chromosome-length scaffolds. After the first round of 3d-dna, we manually adjusted the assembly with Juicebox<sup>54</sup>. Furthermore, to improve the chromosome-scale assembly, we performed 3d-dna within each chromosome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Dudchenko O, Batra SS, Omer AD, Nyquist SK, Hoeger M, Durand NC, et al. De novo assembly of the aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science. 2017;356:925." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3933">78</a>]. The chromosome-length assembly was further improved by manual adjustment with Juicebox [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Durand NC, Robinson JT, Shamim MS, Machol I, Mesirov JP, Lander ES, et al. Juicebox provides a visualization system for Hi-C contact maps with unlimited zoom. Cell Syst. 2016;3:99101." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3936">79</a>], including correcting mis-joins, changing the order and orientation of draft scaffolds with translocation and inversion errors, and adjusting chromosome boundaries (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#MOESM1">2</a>). Finally, we performed LR-Gapcloser [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Xu GC, Xu TJ, Zhu R, Zhang Y, Li SQ, Wang HW, et al. LR_Gapcloser: A tiling pathbased gap closer that uses long reads to complete genome assembly. Gigascience. 2019;8: &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/gigascience/giy157&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3943">33</a>] with PacBio long reads to close gaps (in the assembly) two times, and pilon [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Walker B, Abeel T, Shea T, Priest M, Abouelliel A, Sakthikumar S, et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One. 2014;9:e112963." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3946">76</a>] with Illumina paired-end short reads five times to improve the assembly. Redundans was used to remove redundancy in the unplaced contigs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Pryszcz LP, Gabaldon T. Redundans: an assembly pipeline for highly heterozygous genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2016;44:e113." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3949">80</a>].</p><p>To evaluate the quality of the assembled genome, we mapped the Illumina paired-end reads, PacBio long reads, and RNA-seq reads to the genome using BWA-MEM [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Li H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. arXiv. 2013:arXiv:1303.3997v2." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3955">81</a>], Minimap2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Li H. Minimap2: pairwise alignment for nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics. 2018;34:3094100." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3958">82</a>], and hisat2, respectively. We also used BUSCO to examine the completeness of gene content with the Embryophyta odb9 database and default parameters [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Simao FR, Waterhouse M, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:32102." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3961">34</a>]. The LAI program was used to assess the quality of the assembly [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Ou S, Chen J, Jiang N. Assessing genome assembly quality using the LTR assembly index (LAI). Nucleic Acids Res. 2018;46:e126." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3964">83</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">RNA library construction and sequencing</h3><p>We performed RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) to improve the prediction of gene models. Different plant tissues from <i>T. sinense</i>, including bud, stem, leaf, fruit, phloem, and xylem, were collected and frozen in liquid nitrogen. Total RNA was extracted with the RNAprep Pure Plant Kit (Tiangen Biotech Co., Ltd.) according to the manufacturers instructions. Transcriptome libraries were constructed using NEBNext Ultra RNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina (New England Biolabs Inc.) following the manufacturers procedure. Then, 150-bp paired-end sequencing was performed using the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Transcriptome assembly</h3><p>Three approaches were used to assemble the transcriptome of <i>T. sinense</i>. First, RNA-seq reads from six selected tissues were de novo assembled into transcripts with Trinity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011;29:64452." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3989">84</a>]. In the second and third approaches, RNA-seq reads were first mapped to the assembled genome of <i>T. sinense</i> using hisat2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Kim D, Langmead B, Salzberg SL. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat Methods. 2015;12:35760." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3995">85</a>]. Then, they were assembled into transcripts using the genome-guided strategy by Trinity and StringTie [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Pertea M, Pertea GM, Antonescu CM, Chang TC, Mendell JT, Salzberg SL. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat Biotechnol. 2015;33:2905." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d33374e3998">86</a>]. Transcripts obtained from different approaches were then merged and redundant transcripts were removed with a cutoff of 95% in both identity and coverage with CD-HIT [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Fu L, Niu B, Zhu Z, Wu S, Li W. CD-HIT: accelerated for clustering the next-generation sequencing data. Bioinformatics. 2012;28:31502." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d33374e4002">87</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Genome annotation</h3><p>The library of repeat families was generated from our assembly using RepeatModeler [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Price AL, Jones NC, Pevzner PA. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics. 2005;21:i3518." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d33374e4013">88</a>]. Then with this repeat library, RepeatMasker was used to identify repetitive elements in the genomic sequences. Protein-coding gene prediction of the <i>T. sinense</i> genome was conducted by the MAKER genome annotation pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Holt C, Yandell M. Maker2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:491." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d33374e4019">89</a>], integrating ab initio prediction with de novo assembled transcripts and protein homology data from <i>Nelumbo nucifera</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. Gene functional annotation was performed using BLAT by searching against the Swiss-Prot, TrEMBL, NR, Pfam and eggNOG databases [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Kent WJ. BLATthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 2002;12:65664." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d33374e4029">90</a>], and using InterProScan [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Jones P, Binns D, Chang HY, Fraser M, Li W, McAnulla C, et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:123640." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d33374e4032">91</a>] by searching against protein databases of InterPro. The gene ontology and KEGG information for each gene model was extracted from Swiss-Prot, TrEMBL.</p><p>The tRNA genes were identified by tRNAscan-SE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Lowe TM, Eddy SR. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 1997;25:95564." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d33374e4038">92</a>], rRNA genes were identified by RNAMMER [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Lagesen K, Hallin P, Rdland EA, Staerfeldt HH, Rognes T, Ussery DW. Rnammer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35:31008." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d33374e4041">93</a>], and non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs) genes were identified by searching against the Rfam database [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Kalvari I, Argasinska J, Quinones-Olvera N, Nawrocki EP, Rivas E, Eddy SR, et al. Rfam 13.0: shifting to a genome-centric resource for non-coding RNA families. Nucleic Acids Res. 2018;46:D33542." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d33374e4044">94</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Phylogenetic analysis and estimation of the divergence time of species</h3><p>OrthoFinder v2.3.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Emms DM, Kelly S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 2015;16:157. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1186/s13059-015-0721-2&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02198-7#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d33374e4055">35</a>] was used to generate gene family (orthogroups) classifications from 19 species including two basal angiosperms (<i>Amborella trichopoda</i> and <i>Nymphaea colorata</i>), three magnoliids (<i>Liriodendron chinense</i>, <i>Cinnamomum kanehirae</i>, and <i>Litsea cubeba</i>), four monocots (<i>Musa acuminata</i>, <i>Ananas comosus</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, and <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>), four basal eudicots (<i>Aquilegia coerulea</i>, <i>Nelumbo nucifera</i>, <i>T. sinense</i>, and <i>Trochodendron aralioides</i>), and six eudicots (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, and <i>Coffea canephora</i>). Orthologous groups (OGs) with single-copy genes were used in the phylogenetic analysis.
